- **References:** `<45ab836a.0308031802.2c9685eb@posting.google.com> <796f488f.0308042203.641afe64@posting.google.com> <bha4np$9lm$00$1@news.t-online.com> <3f38cfd6_3@news.athenanews.com>`

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in
news:3f38cfd6_3@news.athenanews.com: 

> 
> "Dr Engelbert Buxbaum" <engelbert_buxbaum@hotmail.com> wrote in
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> provide them with computer solutions to business problems.

Unfortunately, the amount of people I meet who respond with something 
like "Oh, I don't know anything about computers." whenever I'm introduced 
to new groups of people is quite distressing. They seem to revel in their 
lack of ability to use a company, telling me about all the silly things 
they do like typing memos in a spreadsheet package. 

You're right: these people are not about to become computer programmers. 
They have about as much wish to learn how to use a computer more than is 
exactly required for them to keep their job as I do to be one of them.

These people don't drive the market: they 'enable' it's stagnatation. 
Companies who target them make the same old stuff and find ever more 
inventive ways to sell it to people, most of whom don't really need it 
but are convinced that they do.

The people who you refer to do exist, but they're certainly not a large 
enough market to bother catering for many companies, some who are 
believed to be supreme in our field. The actual 'geeks' who are above 
them in computer literacy, many of them programmers, want to see changes 
and quickly but they're not sufficient to sway the 'general' market to 
their will. Even together, the 'power users' and 'the geeks' are 
virtually drowned out by the point-and-drool masses who band together 
with their mantra of "We know nothing.".

> The Marketplace calls the shots.
> 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> of this Century, MAYBE, it is early days yet... <G>)

Unless Computer Intelligence (rather than AI) is perfected, and then 
widely accepted, I can't see this future coming to pass quite so quickly. 
:)

Instead of 2015, I'm going to look at 2010 a mere 7 years from now and, 
for reference, I'm going to use 1996.

Since 1996 there have been some changes for the 'majority' of large 
computer users. The 'hot' OSs now are a little different, and they're 
bundled with a few more toys which reflect the hardware in a standard 
retail PC. The kinds of things computers are used for haven't much 
changed since 1996 except for increased popularity.

Quite a few things have changed at the back end of systems the end users 
interact with, as the inevitable waxing and waning of technologies 
continues. To be honest, I'd be surprised if anyone other than the geeks 
and perhaps 1 or 2 levels of management directly above them actually 
care.

Fast forward seven years from now, based on where we were 7 years ago, I 
can't really see such sweeping changes being likely. Yes, there will be 
changes, but I certainly think it extremely unlikely that the programming 
profession will have been drastically shrunk in importance or usefulness.

> Procedural code is already into Gotterdammerung. It takes too long,
> requires too much skill, is too inflexible (the accelerating rate of
> change in the Marketplace and in technology is another reason why it
> is doomed to extinction) and, overall, costs far too much.

As always, it all depends.

Company X makes a product which contains software and suitable hardware 
to run that software. The software is essentially a one off cost. The 
hardware has a cost per unit.

If I make a million units at a manufacturing cost of �30 per unit (which, 
is an absolute pittance) that's �30,000,000. Now, is it worth trimming 
off �4 a unit by using a less powerful processor which is harder to write 
software for? Well, harder in this case means more expensive: you need 
more people who are more skilled. Is it going to be �4,000,000 more 
expensive?

In some circumstances it's better to use a more efficient language even 
though it takes more skill, higher paid employees and more of them to get 
the job done on time.

The only way that it's 'doomed to extinction' is if you can find a way of 
dropping the unit cost even further - especially if you were also 
dropping the development cost.

I really can't see this changing for quite some time, though change it 
will. How, precisely, it will change I've no idea since it's more to do 
with hardware than it is with software.

Outside of this sort of arena, procedural code has already waned taken 
over by it's slightly newer and 'better' cousin.

> For the last 5 decades Commerce and Industry (Scientific computing is
> a horse of different colour, and is excluded from this discussion...)
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> amounts being spent on in-house IT.

Good. :) Perhaps they'll have the sense to get off the 'forced 
obsolescence' cycle where working, useful and well used software (and 
hardware) is abolished in favour of something new, buggy and where the 
staff need a year or more to be fully retrained in it's use.

It might also reduce the amount of pointless computerisation which goes 
on, where the cost of setting up and maintaning (and replacing every 3-5 
years) completely obliterates whatever financial advantages such systems 
are supposed to provide.

> As fast as they get people trained
> they either leave or need new skills... Why bother? Why should an
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> offshore outsourcing, but it is unlikely.

Ah, but there is still a market for it - and one which is perhaps growing 
well. That it isn't quite so prevalent in your desktop or your companies 
network doesn't mean it's not prevalent in the other 9 computers in your 
room and about your person.
 
> Bottom Line: Don't get smug about COBOL dying and PASCAL surviving;
> they are on the same parachute and the ground is coming up....
> 
> Pete.
 
All languages and tools go through different forms. Ultimately, a 
programming language is a tool for performing a task. That now I may use 
language X rather than language Y is no different than any other 
technological progress: circumstances change and the tools of the trade 
are adapted to meet that change.

Nails are still nails, and hammers are still hammers. They meet a need 
and meet it well. Their shape and materials have changed a lot, but the 
basic function and concept is the same. It's no different with any other 
tool, be it an object you can find in your toolbox hiding under the 
socket set or a virtual thing like a programming language. They will 
change with their requirements but they're still fundamentally the same.

Ian Woods
```

```
Are you saying that in 20 years, a programmer wont have the tools to make
his own programming language, his own OS should he or she decide to?  And
they call that progress?  I call it going backwards here.   If this is what
I'm gonna face in 20 years, I'll be making endless copies of DOS Linux and
maybe OS/2 so that I have the choice to do what I want.  (note that I didn't
mention Windows ;-)

Here's my view of things, from my point of view, so you can't sue me for
saying this...hehehe.

We haven't even begun to touch the tip of the iceburg into what we can do as
far as software development goes.  And while microsoft seems to be amazed by
it's Windows, Where it's been, where it is now, and where's it's going to
be, since about 3 to 4 thousand programmers went into the making of Windows,
I'm not impressed by those results.  This having been said, So far, all
we've done for all these decades, is make the computer do that we dont want
to do.  (Hefty calculations, any repetitive tasks, games (not for the same
reasons of course :-).  But we haven't even begun to tap into the potential
that's ahead of us.

To me What you are suggesting is that we let the others come up with the new
stuff, give the users the ability adjust/change what the user did through
the use of somewhat flexible modules, and that's it for the programmer?  I'm
thinking much longer term than that.  After this step of yours happens, do
you really think that everything will have been made that can be made in the
whole computer industry?  I beg to differ, as this approach the the future
of computing is one of many thousands of avenues, and I'm not saying there's
only that way out of it, even if this ever gets made, it wont close the door
to the rest of the potentials that still are, to date, untouched.

But that's my vision of it, once your implementation exists and is stable,
do you think the users, ever so evolving as you say (which I do have to
agree that they are) will stay contended with this?  that they wont want
more?  Give a man an inch, he'll take foot, etc etc etc....I dont seen that
human behavior stopping anytime soon.  To stop that human behavior, we might
as well stop populating since after 5 billion people we can safely assume
we've conceived every possible kind of human being?  not at all :-).  far
from it.  And the same goes for programming.  Your view is one of many
parralel views, views that will all equally evolve, each in their own
specific ways, each towards very specific and unique goals.  And as long as
they are computers, there will be programmers.  And programming languages
that will range from low level to high level.  The way Pascal is adjusting
to the current reality of development, I dont fear that it can adapt to any
new programming concept we can throw at it.  It's been doign great at
adapting thus far.

Remember, software development is not a user only oriented concept. :-) at
least not in my book.

And that's my 0.02 cents worth :-)....(ok maybe there's a couple dollars in
there instead :-).
```

in the future. I believe it won't be by Procedural code. (There are many
reasons for this... and I am NOT suggesting that NO-ONE will be writing
procedural code. It just won't be commercially viable to develop business
solutions in this way. In fact, it isn't even today. It's just that there
has been no alternative for 40 years...)

> To me What you are suggesting is that we let the others come up with the
new
> stuff, give the users the ability adjust/change what the user did through

I'm

No, that is pushing my argument beyond its limits and I couldn't defend such
a position. I am not speculating on  the "whole computer industry", only
commercial systems development.


> I beg to differ, as this approach the the future
> of computing is one of many thousands of avenues, and I'm not saying
there's
> only that way out of it, even if this ever gets made, it wont close the
door
> to the rest of the potentials that still are, to date, untouched.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> more?  Give a man an inch, he'll take foot, etc etc etc....I dont seen
that
> human behavior stopping anytime soon.  To stop that human behavior, we
might
> as well stop populating since after 5 billion people we can safely assume
> we've conceived every possible kind of human being?  not at all :-).  far
> from it.  And the same goes for programming.  Your view is one of many
> parralel views, views that will all equally evolve, each in their own
> specific ways, each towards very specific and unique goals.  And as long
as
> they are computers, there will be programmers.

The question is whether those "programmers" will be human... We already have
computers that monitor computers. In heuristic systems, programs modify
themselves so fast that the only way to know what happened is to monitor it
with another computer. While this is outside the arena I have defined for my
speculation, it is interesting and fun to look at.

I see a time when some programs will attain a result (based on trial and
error and a program modifying itself several trillion times within a few
minutes) where it will not be (humanly) possible to know HOW it arrived at
the solution. We'll just be thankful we HAVE a solution. There will be no
way of knowing whether the solution is optimum. The best we could do is run
the program again and hope for a shorter result...

This means a computer will attain a result and NO-ONE will know HOW it did
it.

As a Programmer, you may find this an exciting prospect, or you may find it
terrifying. (personally, I'm in the former category). No matter how you feel
about it, it will happen (has already, actually...).

Your statement regarding computers and programmers may be suspect.


> And programming languages
> that will range from low level to high level.  The way Pascal is adjusting
> to the current reality of development, I dont fear that it can adapt to
any
> new programming concept we can throw at it.  It's been doign great at
> adapting thus far.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

in

Pete.

"Stephane Richard" <stephane.richard@verizon.net> wrote in message

A few comments:
I don't have 100% control now, when I give an assignment to another
programmer, I don't know what I'm going to get. The solution may be
effective, and efficient, but experience tells me it may also be neither.

As time goes on, I find I go into my bag of tricks less frequently.
I can't justify going the extra mile when a solution that is less than
optimal runs in an acceptable time frame.
Remember machines are getting faster, and larger, and sometimes the solution
for an inefficient programs is "buy a faster machine".

It will be the same with system modeling.

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.lang.c,comp.lang.c++,comp.lang.java.programmer,comp.lang.pascal.ansi-iso

- **References:** `<45ab836a.0308031802.2c9685eb@posting.google.com> <796f488f.0308042203.641afe64@posting.google.com> <bha4np$9lm$00$1@news.t-online.com> <3f38cfd6_3@news.athenanews.com> <HcB_a.2659$jw4.1195@nwrdny03.gnilink.net> <3f3c207c_4@corp-news.newsgroups.com> <oxX_a.18673$UB4.13645@nwrdny01.gnilink.net> <Ef3%a.101423$0v4.7002733@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```

"Harley" <dennis.harleyNoSpam@worldnet.att.net> wrote in message
news:Ef3%a.101423$0v4.7002733@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net...
>
<snip>

> In the past we have had "user friendly" tools, and the user never used
them.
> It will be the same with system modeling.
> There will be modeling "experts" who do the work.
>
Only if we use modelling.

Suppose they just build something like what they are after, and then refine
it until it does what they want? (See the sample dialogue I posted between a
Businessman and a computer). There are many classes of problem where it
ISN'T necessary to model. In fact, modelling is more properly a part of the
"design" school rather than the "evolve" school.

But, in the interests of a lively discussion <G>, let's say you're right on
that.

So the modeller does the model and works with the user and they jointly
refine it.

How long do you think it would be before the user is capable of doing the
modelling?

(In other words, he (the user) has learned how to do the modelling by
watching someone do it, in a context which he (the user) completely
understands because it is HIS application requirement that is being met.)

It will depend on how complex the modelling tool is, won't it?

This is where the smart software comes in. If the interface is simple and
intuitive, it won't take very long at all.

If you need a 2 month course in order to be able to use the modelling tool,
then we haven't really made much progress.

It is important to recognise that there is a difference between designing
something and then building it (as we currently do, for the most part), and
building something, then iteratively modifying the design until it does what
you want.

Traditionally this latter has been frowned on. It smacks of cardboard and
string and Mickey Mouse solutions. Anyone who's a Programmer will probably
have encountered the "hot shot" who comes in, sits down, and starts coding.
Produces the goods in a day, then when it falls over in production, he or
someone else spends 4 days debugging it because it wasn't "designed"...

Well, that is certainly a good argument for going the "design" approach. (As
a matter of fact, I endorse the "design" approach for manually written
program code...wouldn't argue against it because it has a long, successful,
track record...<G>)

But hold on a minute...we aren't talking about a Human producing code, or
debugging it, or maintaining it.

Instead it will be done by software that is smart enough to do it, using
libraries of known functions and components, selected in response to user
requirements that have been elicited and refined by INTERACTION between the
user and the software.

Only the FUNCTIONALITY needs to be focused on; NOT the programming. Under
these circumstances, the "evolve" method has some outstanding benefits. Not
the least of which is that a Programmer or technically adept "modeller" is
not required. (That may or may not be an advantage, depending on your point
of view, but from a cost accounting POV it is a huge advantage...)

In 15 years I believe we will see users developing their own computer
systems, interfacing with the spreadsheets and databases they are becooming
proficient with, using the evolutionary model methodology, and smart
software to do the "grunt" work.

Power to the People! (And, Freedom for Tooting!).

Pete.
```

"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:3f3cdcfc_1@news.athenanews.com...
|
| "Harley" <dennis.harleyNoSpam@worldnet.att.net> wrote in message
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
| Suppose they just build something like what they are after, and then
refine

a
| Businessman and a computer). There are many classes of problem where it
| ISN'T necessary to model. In fact, modelling is more properly a part of
the
| "design" school rather than the "evolve" school.

This sounds like the Waterfall.
I agree it's a shorter path from one step to the other, but it's the
Waterfall.

I feel that the main benefit of modeling is that the model documents the
current system.
The Waterfall methods that I've seen used, produce mountains of
documentation, that fail to provide a comprehensive definition of the
current system.

It seems that the original system design, if it was documented, is never
ammended as the system progresses through the evolutionary process.

|
| But, in the interests of a lively discussion <G>, let's say you're right
on
| that.
|
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
| modelling?

It isn't capability, it's the desire to do it.

Most of the users I deal with are involved in several projects.
I am usually dealing with several levels of management from more than one
operational area.
They are part of the development team because they want to ensure that their
needs aren't overlooked, and they want to get the maximum return on their
investment.
Everyone provides input in the discussions, and their knowledge, experience,
and stated business goals are essential to the successful delivery of the
final product.
IT, in some respects, is the project co-ordinator.

It's sometimes difficult to schedule a meeting, because I need to find a
common open time on everyone's schedule.
They are involved in other aspects of the business, especially the
management of their respective areas of the business.

If they were to assume the responsibility of modeling, the project would
probably falter due to differing priorities, personnel availability, and,
probably, a lack of co-ordination.

A dedicated modeling staff is needed to address all the issues as a whole,
and consult with individuals on an as needed basis. Inter-dependent
components can be incorporated in the design concurrently, and evaluated in
a more timely manner.
Co-ordination is the key.

In short, there is no "one" user.

| (In other words, he (the user) has learned how to do the modelling by
| watching someone do it, in a context which he (the user) completely

Peter E.C. Dashwood wrote:


> We may have our own favourite Languages and we can poddle away in a corner

> get solutions. By 2015 a new generation of development software will see

> Procedural code is already into Gotterdammerung. It takes too long, requires

I have a different experience. Whenever I have a complicated data
evaluation task, I write a little pascal program to parse the data
(which sometimes come in a strange format out of lab equipment) and do
the necessary calcualtions. I keep a library of common task like
integrating statistical distributions and the like, which can be
recycled.

This is frequently easier than using a spreadsheet for that purpose,
which was supposed to be the easy solution for people who can not or do
not want to programm.

Such programs can be quick and dirty jobs with the charme of a
Unix-filter, as I am the only one using them. For that reason I am even
disappointed by the development that Delphi/Kylix has taken over the
last 10 years: Bigger, more complicated and worse documented. It may
help profesional programmers who need to make nice user interfaces. But
little guys like me who just need a job done were better off with good
old Turbo Pascal.

- **Message-ID:** `<3f42004e_5@news.athenanews.com>`
- **References:** `<45ab836a.0308031802.2c9685eb@posting.google.com> <796f488f.0308042203.641afe64@posting.google.com> <bha4np$9lm$00$1@news.t-online.com> <3f38cfd6_3@news.athenanews.com> <bhsipd$pb8$04$1@news.t-online.com>`

```

"Dr Engelbert Buxbaum" <engelbert_buxbaum@hotmail.com> wrote in message
news:bhsipd$pb8$04$1@news.t-online.com...
> Peter E.C. Dashwood wrote:
>
>
> > We may have our own favourite Languages and we can poddle away in a
corner
> > somewhere cutting code for the fun of it, but the real world demands
that it
> > get solutions. By 2015 a new generation of development software will see
> > "programmers" removed from the loop and end users interacting and
iterating
> > with smart software until they get what they want.
>
> Sounds like something big, clumpsy, slow and limited to some narrow set
> of tasks, that one could just as well do without.
>

Yes, it could sound that way. However, I am talking about a different world
to the one you describe, below.

It is a world where, currently, groups of people in a given business need to
translate their business requirements for computer support into actual
computer code that supports their processes.  As they are not technical,
they need to interface with a technical group (IT). There is a "standard"
methodology for doing this (it is called "The Waterfall") which ensures that
the various development phases are complete, agreed, and signed off. This
methodology is fatally flawed but up until a few years ago there was little
alternative to it. (please see:
http://www.aboutlegacycoding.com/Archives/V3/V30501.asp  and
http://www.aboutlegacycoding.com/Archives/V3/V30201.asp) for the background
to my position on this.)

An alternative methodology is Rapid Application Development (RAD) where the
users work jointly with IT to achieve technical goals. These goals are
jointly identified and "timeboxed". There is far too much to go into here,
but having tried this approach on a number of projects, and found it to be
significantly better than the Waterfall, I got to thinking about WHY it is
better. The keys to its success are ITERATION and INTERACTION.

Very often users don't know what they want, but they know what they DON'T
want. If they can see some "dry runs" or prototypes, it helps them to focus
on what is really important, organise the priorities better, and obtain
systems that are more "up to the minute" in terms of meeting Business
requirement.  So, by INTERACTING with IT in joint workshops, initial
solutions can be obtained and these can then be refined by ITERATION.The
process of doing this is much more responsive than the Waterfall and so the
results obtained are much more likely to refelect the CURRENT Business
requirement.

Currently, we have done this with programmers writing the code. It seems to
me that in 15 years we could have smart software that can generate code in
milliseconds, throw it away and do it again in response to interaction with
a User. (The details of this "generated code" are, again, too much to go
into here, but it is reasonable to suppose that component technology and OO
programming and design will certainly play a part in it (see the links
above). In fact, I believe it is the advent of these two technologies (one
actually an extension of the other) that has made this solution even
possible. This "smart" software will achieve much larger re-use of code than
is currently the case with human programmers.


> > Procedural code is already into Gotterdammerung. It takes too long,
requires
> > too much skill, is too inflexible (the accelerating rate of change in
the
> > Marketplace and in technology is another reason why it is doomed to
> > extinction) and, overall, costs far too much.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> recycled.

Yes, I agree. In the circumstances you describe, there is no reason why you
shouldn't do what you do. I am not suggesting my proposed solution is right
for every context.

>
> This is frequently easier than using a spreadsheet for that purpose,
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> old Turbo Pascal.

Fortunately, you can still use Turbo Pascal (or Delphi or whatever you are
comfortable with...). As you are your own user there probably isn't too much
problem with whatever you use.

There is a HUGE amount behind the position I have adopted and it is
certainly more than I care to post in this forum. I have given links (in
response to requests) that give some insight into where I am coming from and
why I believe the approach proposed will eventually come to fruition...
(basically, it is a logical extrapolation from what I have seen work in
practice in corporations).

I believe that the commercial marketplace will drive the development of such
systems.

Pete.
```

James

How much time will be spect maintaining the code?

If the code willbe stable and not maintained too often, then development
time will be more important than maintenance time.

However, if it will be maintained quite frequently and you don't mind a
longer development time, just go with "C" or Cobol, depending on the type of
application.

If development cost is critical, then just choose any development language
that is not undergoing a lot of chnge at the moment.  Probably one that you
have skills to use already.

Jim

"James Cameron" <james.cameron@bindereng.com.au> schreef in bericht
news:45ab836a.0308031802.2c9685eb@posting.google.com...
> Hi I'm developing a program and the client is worried about future
> reuse of the code. Say 5, 10, 15 years down the road.

How about even 20 years down the road ? One of my former colleagues had a
crush on date handling. Up to now I am still using his code (with no changes
made ever since) in new applications and when maintaining other apps. His
coding even supports the gregorian date change in 1560......I never needed
that however.

That code was written for a NCR IMOS system. In plain ANSII cobol. As you
will read in the other replies on this thread there are some standards such
piece of code needs to meet. Like no user interface (only parameter
handling) and plain standard coding (no proprietry routines should be used).

I have put lots of this kind of routines in Fujitsu Cobol DLL's which makes
them accessable for other languages too....To be frankly honest, I never
looked at the source code of these routines. I dont need to, they work !
(Thanks to Robert Veldwijk).

Regards, Danny.

BTW, When I started programming in COBOL I was warned to upgrade my skills
to other languages (which I did) because COBOL would be dead and gone within
5 years of that moment. That was 1984......The main part of my business is
still COBOL today :)
